## Chapter 2: Application of Newton’s Laws I:

### Introduction

In the previous chapter, we introduced the fundamental principles of classical mechanics, including Newton's laws of motion. These laws form the foundation of classical mechanics and are essential for understanding the motion of objects in the physical world. In this chapter, we will delve deeper into the application of these laws, specifically focusing on the first two laws.

Newton's first law of motion states that an object at rest tends to stay at rest, and an object in motion tends to stay in motion, unless acted upon by an external force. This law is often referred to as the law of inertia and is crucial for understanding the behavior of objects in motion. We will explore this law in more detail and discuss its implications for various physical systems.

Newton's second law of motion states that the force acting on an object is directly proportional to its mass and the rate of change of its velocity. This law is often expressed mathematically as $F = ma$, where $F$ is the force, $m$ is the mass, and $a$ is the acceleration. We will also discuss this law in more detail and explore its applications in various physical systems.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of Newton's laws of motion and their applications in classical mechanics. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the rest of the book, as we continue to explore more advanced topics in classical mechanics. So let's dive in and explore the fascinating world of classical mechanics!




### Section: 2.1 Free Body Diagrams:

In classical mechanics, it is crucial to understand the forces acting on a body in order to analyze its motion. This is where free body diagrams (FBDs) come into play. An FBD is a graphical representation of a body or system, showing all the external forces acting on it. These diagrams are essential for visualizing and analyzing the forces acting on a body, and they are the first step in applying Newton's laws of motion.

#### 2.1a Free Body Diagrams

To construct an FBD, we first need to identify the body or system we want to analyze. This can be done by drawing a box around the body, as shown in Figure 1. Next, we need to identify all the external forces acting on the body. These forces can be contact forces, such as friction or normal force, or non-contact forces, such as gravity or magnetic force. We can represent these forces as arrows, with the tail of the arrow representing the point of application and the head of the arrow representing the direction of the force.

$$
\Delta w = ...
$$

Figure 1: Free body diagram of a box

Once we have identified all the external forces, we can apply Newton's second law of motion to the body. This law states that the sum of all forces acting on a body is equal to the mass of the body times its acceleration. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\sum F = m \cdot a
$$

where $\sum F$ is the sum of all forces, $m$ is the mass of the body, and $a$ is the acceleration of the body. By analyzing the FBD and applying Newton's second law, we can determine the acceleration of the body and predict its motion.

In addition to analyzing the forces acting on a body, FBDs can also be used to visualize the rotation of a spacecraft in orbit. This is done through Poinsot's construction, which uses a geometric representation to visualize the rotation of a spacecraft. By understanding the forces acting on a spacecraft, we can use Poinsot's construction to visualize its rotation and predict its motion.

Further reading on FBDs can be found in the publications of P. K. Agarwal, L. J. Guibas, J. Hershberger, and E. Verach. These authors have made significant contributions to the field of computational geometry, which is closely related to FBDs. Their work has been applied to various problems, including maintaining the extent of a moving set of points.

In conclusion, free body diagrams are an essential tool in classical mechanics. They allow us to visualize and analyze the forces acting on a body, and they are the first step in applying Newton's laws of motion. By understanding the forces acting on a body, we can predict its motion and gain a deeper understanding of the physical world. 





### Section: 2.1b Equilibrium

In classical mechanics, equilibrium is a state in which all forces acting on a body are balanced, resulting in no net force or acceleration. This concept is crucial in understanding the behavior of objects in motion and is closely related to Newton's laws of motion.

#### 2.1b.1 Conditions for Equilibrium

For a body to be in equilibrium, the sum of all forces acting on it must be equal to zero. This can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
\sum F = 0
$$

Additionally, the sum of all torques acting on the body must also be equal to zero. This can be expressed as:

$$
\sum \tau = 0
$$

where $\tau$ represents the torque. Torque is a measure of the rotational force acting on a body and is defined as the product of the force and the distance from the axis of rotation. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\tau = r \times F
$$

where $r$ is the distance from the axis of rotation and $F$ is the force.

#### 2.1b.2 Equilibrium of a Rigid Body

For a rigid body, equilibrium can be achieved in three ways: translation, rotation, and general equilibrium. In translation equilibrium, the body is at rest or moving at a constant velocity, and there are no torques acting on it. In rotation equilibrium, the body is rotating at a constant angular velocity, and there are no forces acting on it. In general equilibrium, the body is both at rest and rotating at a constant velocity, and there are no forces or torques acting on it.

#### 2.1b.3 Equilibrium of a Non-Rigid Body

For a non-rigid body, equilibrium can be achieved in two ways: static equilibrium and dynamic equilibrium. In static equilibrium, the body is at rest or moving at a constant velocity, and there are no torques acting on it. In dynamic equilibrium, the body is moving at a constant velocity, and there are no forces acting on it.

#### 2.1b.4 Equilibrium of a System of Bodies

For a system of bodies, equilibrium can be achieved in two ways: stable equilibrium and unstable equilibrium. In stable equilibrium, all bodies in the system are in equilibrium, and any small disturbance will result in the system returning to equilibrium. In unstable equilibrium, some bodies in the system are not in equilibrium, and any small disturbance will result in the system moving further away from equilibrium.

#### 2.1b.5 Equilibrium of a System of Forces

For a system of forces, equilibrium can be achieved in two ways: static equilibrium and dynamic equilibrium. In static equilibrium, the sum of all forces acting on the system is equal to zero, and there are no torques acting on the system. In dynamic equilibrium, the sum of all forces acting on the system is equal to the mass of the system times its acceleration, and there are no torques acting on the system.

#### 2.1b.6 Equilibrium of a System of Moments

For a system of moments, equilibrium can be achieved in two ways: static equilibrium and dynamic equilibrium. In static equilibrium, the sum of all moments acting on the system is equal to zero, and there are no forces acting on the system. In dynamic equilibrium, the sum of all moments acting on the system is equal to the moment of inertia of the system times its angular acceleration, and there are no forces acting on the system.

#### 2.1b.7 Equilibrium of a System of Forces and Moments

For a system of forces and moments, equilibrium can be achieved in two ways: static equilibrium and dynamic equilibrium. In static equilibrium, the sum of all forces and moments acting on the system is equal to zero, and there are no torques acting on the system. In dynamic equilibrium, the sum of all forces and moments acting on the system is equal to the mass of the system times its acceleration and the moment of inertia of the system times its angular acceleration, and there are no torques acting on the system.





### Section: 2.1c Tension

Tension is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics, particularly in the study of forces and motion. It is defined as the force exerted by a stretched string, wire, or cable. Tension is a vector quantity, meaning it has both magnitude and direction. In this section, we will explore the concept of tension and its role in classical mechanics.

#### 2.1c.1 Tension in a String

Consider a string of length $L$ with a mass $m$ attached to one end and a force $F$ applied to the other end. The string is stretched by a distance $x$, and we want to find the tension $T$ in the string. By Newton's second law, the force acting on the mass $m$ is equal to the product of its mass and acceleration. Since the mass is moving at a constant velocity, its acceleration is zero, and the force acting on it is equal to zero. This means that the tension $T$ in the string is equal to the force $F$ applied to the other end of the string. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = F
$$

#### 2.1c.2 Tension in a System of Strings

In a system of strings, the tension in each string can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ strings, each with a mass $m$ attached to one end and a force $F$ applied to the other end. The strings are stretched by a distance $x$, and we want to find the total tension $T$ in the system. The total tension is equal to the sum of the tensions in each individual string. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = nT
$$

#### 2.1c.3 Tension in a System of Strings with Variable Masses

In a system of strings with variable masses, the tension in each string can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ strings, each with a mass $m_i$ attached to one end and a force $F$ applied to the other end. The strings are stretched by a distance $x$, and we want to find the total tension $T$ in the system. The total tension is equal to the sum of the tensions in each individual string. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^{n} T_i
$$

where $T_i$ is the tension in the $i$th string.

#### 2.1c.4 Tension in a System of Strings with Variable Forces

In a system of strings with variable forces, the tension in each string can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ strings, each with a mass $m$ attached to one end and a force $F_i$ applied to the other end. The strings are stretched by a distance $x$, and we want to find the total tension $T$ in the system. The total tension is equal to the sum of the tensions in each individual string. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^{n} T_i
$$

where $T_i$ is the tension in the $i$th string.

#### 2.1c.5 Tension in a System of Strings with Variable Masses and Forces

In a system of strings with variable masses and forces, the tension in each string can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ strings, each with a mass $m_i$ attached to one end and a force $F_i$ applied to the other end. The strings are stretched by a distance $x$, and we want to find the total tension $T$ in the system. The total tension is equal to the sum of the tensions in each individual string. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^{n} T_i
$$

where $T_i$ is the tension in the $i$th string.

#### 2.1c.6 Tension in a System of Strings with Variable Masses and Forces

In a system of strings with variable masses and forces, the tension in each string can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ strings, each with a mass $m_i$ attached to one end and a force $F_i$ applied to the other end. The strings are stretched by a distance $x$, and we want to find the total tension $T$ in the system. The total tension is equal to the sum of the tensions in each individual string. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^{n} T_i
$$

where $T_i$ is the tension in the $i$th string.




### Section: 2.1d Inclined Planes

Inclined planes are a fundamental concept in classical mechanics, particularly in the study of forces and motion. They are used to model a variety of physical phenomena, such as the motion of objects on ramps or the forces acting on a body in a gravitational field. In this section, we will explore the concept of inclined planes and their role in classical mechanics.

#### 2.1d.1 Inclined Plane with a Constant Angle

Consider an inclined plane with a constant angle $\theta$ between the plane and the horizontal. A body of mass $m$ is placed on the plane at a distance $x$ from the top of the plane. The body is subject to a force $F$ acting parallel to the plane. We want to find the normal force $N$ acting on the body.

By Newton's second law, the force acting on the body is equal to the product of its mass and acceleration. Since the body is moving at a constant velocity, its acceleration is zero, and the force acting on it is equal to zero. This means that the normal force $N$ acting on the body is equal to the product of its mass and the component of the gravitational force acting perpendicular to the plane. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
N = mg\sin(\theta)
$$

#### 2.1d.2 Inclined Plane with a Variable Angle

In a system of inclined planes with variable angles, the normal force in each plane can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ inclined planes, each with a mass $m$ placed on it at a distance $x$ from the top of the plane. The planes have angles $\theta_i$ between the plane and the horizontal. The body is subject to a force $F$ acting parallel to the plane. The normal force $N_i$ acting on the body in the $i$-th plane is equal to the product of its mass and the component of the gravitational force acting perpendicular to the plane. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
N_i = mg\sin(\theta_i)
$$

#### 2.1d.3 Inclined Plane with Friction

In real-world applications, friction is often present on inclined planes. Friction acts parallel to the plane and opposes the motion of the body. The friction force $f$ is equal to the product of the normal force $N$ and the coefficient of friction $\mu$. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
f = \mu N
$$

In the next section, we will explore the concept of friction in more detail and discuss how it affects the motion of bodies on inclined planes.




#### 2.2a Pulleys and Tension

Pulleys are a fundamental tool in classical mechanics, particularly in the study of forces and motion. They are used to change the direction of a force, to lift heavy objects, and to control the speed of a moving object. In this section, we will explore the concept of pulleys and their role in classical mechanics.

#### 2.2a.1 Pulleys and Tension

A pulley is a simple machine that consists of a wheel with a groove around its edge. A rope or cable is passed through the groove and over a fixed object, such as a beam or a hook. The pulley can be used to lift heavy objects by applying a force to the rope or cable. The force applied to the rope or cable is equal to the weight of the object being lifted.

The tension in a pulley system can be calculated using Newton's second law. The tension in a rope or cable is equal to the product of its mass and acceleration. Since the rope or cable is at rest, its acceleration is zero, and the tension is equal to the product of its mass and the component of the gravitational force acting along the rope or cable. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = m g \sin(\theta)
$$

where $T$ is the tension, $m$ is the mass of the rope or cable, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\theta$ is the angle between the rope or cable and the horizontal.

#### 2.2a.2 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In a system of pulleys, the tension in each pulley can be calculated using the same principle. Consider a system of $n$ pulleys, each with a mass $m$ placed on it at a distance $x$ from the top of the pulley. The pulleys have angles $\theta_i$ between the pulley and the horizontal. The pulleys are subject to a force $F$ acting along the pulley. The tension $T_i$ acting on the pulley is equal to the product of its mass and the component of the gravitational force acting along the pulley. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T_i = m g \sin(\theta_i)
$$

#### 2.2a.3 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In real-world applications, friction is often a significant factor in pulley systems. Friction can cause the pulley to slow down or even stop moving. The friction force can be calculated using Coulomb's law, which states that the friction force is equal to the product of the normal force and the coefficient of friction. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
f = \mu N
$$

where $f$ is the friction force, $\mu$ is the coefficient of friction, and $N$ is the normal force.

In a system of pulleys, the friction force can be calculated for each pulley. The total friction force is equal to the sum of the friction forces in each pulley. This can be expressed as:

$$
F = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \mu_i m_i g \sin(\theta_i)
$$

where $F$ is the total friction force, $\mu_i$ is the coefficient of friction for the $i$-th pulley, $m_i$ is the mass of the $i$-th pulley, and $\theta_i$ is the angle between the $i$-th pulley and the horizontal.

#### 2.2a.4 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In a system of pulleys, the tension in each pulley can be calculated using the same principle as before. However, the friction force must also be taken into account. The total tension in each pulley is equal to the sum of the tension due to the gravitational force and the friction force. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T_i = m g \sin(\theta_i) + \mu_i m_i g \sin(\theta_i)
$$

where $T_i$ is the total tension in the $i$-th pulley, $m_i$ is the mass of the $i$-th pulley, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\theta_i$ is the angle between the $i$-th pulley and the horizontal.

#### 2.2a.5 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In a system of pulleys, the total tension can be calculated by summing the total tension in each pulley. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
T = \sum_{i=1}^{n} T_i
$$

where $T$ is the total tension, and $T_i$ is the total tension in the $i$-th pulley.

#### 2.2a.6 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In a system of pulleys, the total friction force can be calculated by summing the friction force in each pulley. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
F = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \mu_i m_i g \sin(\theta_i)
$$

where $F$ is the total friction force, and $\mu_i$, $m_i$, and $\theta_i$ are as defined previously.

#### 2.2a.7 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In a system of pulleys, the total friction force can be reduced by reducing the coefficient of friction. This can be achieved by lubricating the pulleys or by using materials with lower coefficients of friction. The total friction force can also be reduced by reducing the angle between the pulley and the horizontal. This can be achieved by adjusting the position of the pulley or by using pulleys with larger radii.

#### 2.2a.8 Pulleys and Tension with Friction

In a system of pulleys, the total friction force can be reduced by reducing the mass of the pulley. This can be achieved by using lighter materials for the pulley or by reducing the size of the pulley. The total friction force can also be reduced by reducing the angle between the pulley and the horizontal. This can be achieved by adjusting the position of the pulley or by using pulleys with larger radii.

This can be achieved by using lighter materials for the pulley or by reducing the size of the pulley.

#### 2.2b Springs and Hooke's Law

Springs are another fundamental tool in classical mechanics, particularly in the study of forces and motion. They are used to store and release energy, to control the speed of a moving object, and to model the behavior of many physical systems. In this section, we will explore the concept of springs and their role in classical mechanics.

#### 2.2b.1 Springs and Hooke's Law

A spring is a simple machine that stores energy in the form of elastic potential energy. When a spring is compressed or stretched, it stores energy. When the spring is released, this energy is released and can be used to do work. The amount of energy stored in a spring is proportional to the square of the displacement from its equilibrium position. This relationship is described by Hooke's Law, which states that the force $F$ exerted by a spring is given by the equation:

$$
F = -k x
$$

where $k$ is the spring constant, $F$ is the force exerted by the spring, and $x$ is the displacement from the spring's equilibrium position. The negative sign indicates that the force exerted by the spring is always in the direction opposite to the displacement.

#### 2.2b.2 Hooke's Law and Energy Storage

The energy stored in a spring can be calculated using the equation for the potential energy of a spring, which is given by the equation:

$$
U = \frac{1}{2} k x^2
$$

where $U$ is the potential energy stored in the spring, $k$ is the spring constant, and $x$ is the displacement from the spring's equilibrium position. This equation shows that the energy stored in a spring is proportional to the square of the displacement from its equilibrium position.

#### 2.2b.3 Hooke's Law and Oscillations

Hooke's Law is also used to describe the motion of a spring-mass system. When a mass $m$ is attached to a spring with spring constant $k$, the system will oscillate with a natural frequency given by the equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{\frac{k}{m}}
$$

where $\omega$ is the natural frequency of the oscillation. This equation shows that the natural frequency of the oscillation is proportional to the square root of the ratio of the spring constant to the mass.

#### 2.2b.4 Hooke's Law and Nonlinear Systems

While Hooke's Law is often used to describe the behavior of springs, it is not always accurate. In particular, it assumes that the spring constant $k$ is constant, which is not always the case. In many systems, the spring constant can vary with the displacement from the spring's equilibrium position. This can lead to nonlinear behavior, which can be described using more advanced equations such as the Föppl–von Kármán equations.

#### 2.2b.5 Hooke's Law and Energy Dissipation

In real-world systems, energy is not always conserved. In many systems, there are dissipative forces that cause energy to be lost to the environment. This can be modeled using the Föppl–von Kármán equations, which include terms for the dissipation of energy.

#### 2.2b.6 Hooke's Law and Nonlinear Systems

This can lead to nonlinear behavior, which can be described using more advanced equations such as the Föppl–von Kármán equations.

#### 2.2b.61 Hooke's Law and Energy Dissipation

In real-world systems, energy is not always conserved. In many systems, there are dissipative forces that cause energy to be lost to the environment. This can be modeled using the Föppl–von Kármán equations, which include terms for the dissipation of energy.

#### 2.2b.62 Hooke's Law and Nonlinear Systems

#### 2.2c Circular Motion and Centripetal Force

In the previous sections, we have explored the concepts of springs and Hooke's Law. Now, we will delve into the fascinating world of circular motion and the centripetal force.

#### 2.2c.1 Circular Motion

Circular motion is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics. It refers to the motion of an object moving along a circular path. The object's speed and direction are constantly changing, but its speed is always equal to the magnitude of its velocity. This is a direct consequence of the definition of circular motion.

The position of a point moving in a circular motion can be described using a vector $\mathbf{r}(t)$, where $t$ is time. The direction of $\mathbf{r}(t)$ is determined by the right-hand rule: if you curl the fingers of your right hand in the direction of motion, your thumb points in the direction of $\mathbf{r}(t)$.

#### 2.2c.2 Centripetal Force

The centripetal force is the force that keeps an object moving in a circular motion. It is directed towards the center of the circle and is proportional to the square of the object's speed and the inverse of the radius of the circle. This relationship is described by the equation:

$$
F_c = \frac{mv^2}{r}
$$

where $F_c$ is the centripetal force, $m$ is the mass of the object, $v$ is its speed, and $r$ is the radius of the circle.

#### 2.2c.3 Centripetal Force and Newton's Second Law

The centripetal force is a special case of Newton's Second Law of Motion. It states that the force acting on an object is equal to the mass of the object times its acceleration. In the case of circular motion, the acceleration is always directed towards the center of the circle and is equal to the square of the object's speed divided by the radius of the circle. This is why the centripetal force is proportional to the square of the object's speed and the inverse of the radius of the circle.

#### 2.2c.4 Non-uniform Circular Motion

In the previous sections, we have assumed that the angular rate of rotation is constant. However, in many real-world scenarios, this is not the case. For example, a satellite orbiting the Earth experiences a non-uniform circular motion due to the Earth's gravitational field.

In the case of non-uniform circular motion, the acceleration has a tangential component, as shown in the figure. This case is used to demonstrate a derivation strategy based on a polar coordinate system.

The position of a point mass as a function of time is described by a vector $\mathbf{r}(t)$, where $r$ is the radius of the circle and $u_r$ is the unit vector pointing from the origin to the point mass. The direction of $u_r$ is described by the angle $\theta$ between the x-axis and the unit vector, measured counterclockwise from the x-axis.

The velocity of the point mass is given by the derivative of $\mathbf{r}(t)$ with respect to time. This can be calculated using the chain rule:

$$
\mathbf{v} = \frac{dr}{dt}u_r + r\frac{d\theta}{dt}u_\theta
$$

where $u_\theta$ is the unit vector perpendicular to $u_r$ and pointing in the direction of increasing $\theta$.

The acceleration of the point mass is given by the derivative of $\mathbf{v}$ with respect to time:

$$
\mathbf{a} = \frac{d^2r}{dt^2}u_r + 2\frac{dr}{dt}\frac{d\theta}{dt}u_r + r\frac{d^2\theta}{dt^2}u_\theta
$$

The tangential acceleration is given by the second term on the right-hand side of this equation. It is proportional to the product of the tangential velocity and the angular acceleration. This is a direct consequence of the definition of angular acceleration.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of angular acceleration and its relationship with the centripetal force in more detail.




#### 2.2d Banked Curves

In the previous sections, we have explored the concepts of circular motion and the centripetal force. Now, we will delve into the fascinating world of banked curves, a concept that is fundamental to the design of race tracks and roller coasters.

#### 2.2d.1 Banked Curves

A banked curve is a curve on which a vehicle can travel at a speed greater than the speed at which it could travel around a curve of the same radius on a flat surface. This is achieved by tilting the curve, creating a centripetal force that is less than the force of gravity acting on the vehicle. This allows the vehicle to travel around the curve at a higher speed than it could on a flat surface.

The radius of a banked curve can be calculated using the equation:

$$
R = \frac{v^2}{g(1 + k)}
$$

where $R$ is the radius of the curve, $v$ is the speed of the vehicle, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the curvature of the curve.

#### 2.2d.2 Centripetal Force and Banked Curves

The centripetal force acting on a vehicle traveling around a banked curve is given by the equation:

$$
F_c = mg(1 - k)
$$

where $F_c$ is the centripetal force, $m$ is the mass of the vehicle, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the curvature of the curve.

This equation shows that the centripetal force is less than the force of gravity acting on the vehicle. This is why a vehicle can travel around a banked curve at a higher speed than it could on a flat surface.

#### 2.2d.3 Banked Curves and Newton's Second Law

The centripetal force acting on a vehicle traveling around a banked curve is also a special case of Newton's Second Law of Motion. It states that the force acting on an object is equal to the mass of the object times its acceleration. In the case of a banked curve, the acceleration is always directed towards the center of the curve and is equal to the product of the speed of the vehicle, the curvature of the curve, and the acceleration due to gravity. This is why the centripetal force is less than the force of gravity acting on the vehicle.

#### 2.2d.4 Non-uniform Banked Curves

In the previous sections, we have assumed that the curvature of the banked curve is constant. However, in reality, banked curves can have varying curvature along their length. This can be accounted for by using a more general equation for the centripetal force, which includes a term for the variation of curvature along the curve:

$$
F_c = mg(1 - k) + m\frac{d^2k}{dx^2}v^2
$$

where $d^2k/dx^2$ is the second derivative of the curvature with respect to position along the curve. This equation shows that the centripetal force is affected by the variation of curvature along the curve. This is why banked curves with varying curvature can be designed to allow vehicles to travel at even higher speeds.




#### 2.3a Linear Momentum

Linear momentum is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics. It is a vector quantity that describes the linear motion of an object. The linear momentum of an object is the product of its mass and velocity. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{p} = m \mathbf{v}
$$

where $\mathbf{p}$ is the linear momentum, $m$ is the mass of the object, and $\mathbf{v}$ is the velocity of the object.

#### 2.3a.1 Conservation of Linear Momentum

One of the most important principles in classical mechanics is the conservation of linear momentum. This principle states that the total linear momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external force. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\sum \mathbf{p}_i = \sum \mathbf{p}_f
$$

where $\mathbf{p}_i$ is the initial linear momentum of the system and $\mathbf{p}_f$ is the final linear momentum of the system.

The conservation of linear momentum is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion, which states that the rate of change of linear momentum of an object is directly proportional to the applied force. If no external force is acting on a system, the linear momentum of the system remains constant.

#### 2.3a.2 Linear Momentum in Collision Problems

Collision problems are a common application of the concept of linear momentum. In a collision, two or more objects interact for a short period of time, exchanging energy and momentum. The conservation of linear momentum is a powerful tool in analyzing these collisions.

Consider a two-body collision where two objects of masses $m_1$ and $m_2$ are moving with velocities $\mathbf{v}_1$ and $\mathbf{v}_2$ respectively. If they collide and their final velocities are $\mathbf{v}_1'$ and $\mathbf{v}_2'$, the conservation of linear momentum can be written as:

$$
m_1 \mathbf{v}_1 + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2 = m_1 \mathbf{v}_1' + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the unknown velocities after the collision.

#### 2.3a.3 Linear Momentum in Relativistic Mechanics

In relativistic mechanics, the concept of linear momentum is generalized to account for the effects of special relativity. The relativistic linear momentum of an object is given by the equation:

$$
\mathbf{p} = \frac{m \mathbf{v}}{\sqrt{1 - \frac{v^2}{c^2}}}
$$

where $c$ is the speed of light. This equation shows that the linear momentum of an object increases with its velocity, but only up to the speed of light. Beyond this speed, the linear momentum becomes infinite, which is a consequence of the special theory of relativity.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of angular momentum, another fundamental quantity in classical mechanics.

#### 2.3b Angular Momentum

Angular momentum is another fundamental concept in classical mechanics. It is a vector quantity that describes the rotational motion of an object. The angular momentum of an object is the product of its moment of inertia and its angular velocity. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{L} = I \boldsymbol{\omega}
$$

where $\mathbf{L}$ is the angular momentum, $I$ is the moment of inertia, and $\boldsymbol{\omega}$ is the angular velocity.

#### 2.3b.1 Conservation of Angular Momentum

Similar to linear momentum, the conservation of angular momentum is a fundamental principle in classical mechanics. It states that the total angular momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external torque. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\sum \mathbf{L}_i = \sum \mathbf{L}_f
$$

where $\mathbf{L}_i$ is the initial angular momentum of the system and $\mathbf{L}_f$ is the final angular momentum of the system.

The conservation of angular momentum is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion, which states that the rate of change of angular momentum of an object is directly proportional to the applied torque. If no external torque is acting on a system, the angular momentum of the system remains constant.

#### 2.3b.2 Angular Momentum in Rotational Motion

Rotational motion is a common application of the concept of angular momentum. In rotational motion, an object rotates around a fixed axis. The angular momentum of the object can be calculated using the equation:

$$
\mathbf{L} = I \boldsymbol{\omega}
$$

where $I$ is the moment of inertia and $\boldsymbol{\omega}$ is the angular velocity. The moment of inertia is a measure of an object's resistance to rotational motion and is dependent on the mass distribution of the object.

#### 2.3b.3 Angular Momentum in Collision Problems

Collision problems in rotational motion can be analyzed using the conservation of angular momentum. Consider a system of objects rotating around a fixed axis. If the objects collide and their final angular velocities are $\boldsymbol{\omega}_1'$ and $\boldsymbol{\omega}_2'$, the conservation of angular momentum can be written as:

$$
I_1 \boldsymbol{\omega}_1 + I_2 \boldsymbol{\omega}_2 = I_1 \boldsymbol{\omega}_1' + I_2 \boldsymbol{\omega}_2'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the unknown angular velocities after the collision.

#### 2.3b.4 Angular Momentum in Relativistic Mechanics

In relativistic mechanics, the concept of angular momentum is generalized to account for the effects of special relativity. The relativistic angular momentum of an object is given by the equation:

$$
\mathbf{L} = \frac{I \boldsymbol{\omega}}{\sqrt{1 - \frac{\boldsymbol{\omega}^2 r^2}{c^2}}}
$$

where $r$ is the radius of rotation and $c$ is the speed of light. This equation shows that the angular momentum of an object increases with its angular velocity, but only up to the speed of light. Beyond this speed, the angular momentum becomes infinite, which is a consequence of the special theory of relativity.

#### 2.3c Impulse and Momentum

Impulse and momentum are two fundamental concepts in classical mechanics that are closely related. Impulse is defined as the change in momentum of an object over a period of time. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \Delta \mathbf{p}
$$

where $\mathbf{J}$ is the impulse and $\Delta \mathbf{p}$ is the change in momentum.

#### 2.3c.1 Conservation of Momentum

The conservation of momentum is a fundamental principle in classical mechanics. It states that the total momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external impulse. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\sum \mathbf{p}_i = \sum \mathbf{p}_f
$$

where $\mathbf{p}_i$ is the initial momentum of the system and $\mathbf{p}_f$ is the final momentum of the system.

The conservation of momentum is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion, which states that the rate of change of momentum of an object is directly proportional to the applied impulse. If no external impulse is acting on a system, the momentum of the system remains constant.

#### 2.3c.2 Impulse in Collision Problems

Collision problems are a common application of the concepts of impulse and momentum. In a collision, two or more objects interact for a short period of time, exchanging momentum. The conservation of momentum can be used to analyze these collisions.

Consider a system of objects with initial momenta $\mathbf{p}_1$, $\mathbf{p}_2$, ..., $\mathbf{p}_n$. If they collide and their final momenta are $\mathbf{p}_1'$, $\mathbf{p}_2'$, ..., $\mathbf{p}_n'$, the conservation of momentum can be written as:

$$
\mathbf{p}_1 + \mathbf{p}_2 + \cdots + \mathbf{p}_n = \mathbf{p}_1' + \mathbf{p}_2' + \cdots + \mathbf{p}_n'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the unknown final momenta after the collision.

#### 2.3c.3 Impulse in Relativistic Mechanics

In relativistic mechanics, the concept of impulse is generalized to account for the effects of special relativity. The relativistic impulse is defined as the change in momentum divided by the square root of one minus the square of the ratio of the velocity of the object to the speed of light. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \frac{\Delta \mathbf{p}}{\sqrt{1 - \frac{v^2}{c^2}}}
$$

where $v$ is the velocity of the object and $c$ is the speed of light. This equation shows that the impulse of an object increases with its velocity, but only up to the speed of light. Beyond this speed, the impulse becomes infinite, which is a consequence of the special theory of relativity.

#### 2.3d Angular Impulse and Angular Momentum

Angular impulse and angular momentum are two fundamental concepts in classical mechanics that are closely related. Angular impulse is defined as the change in angular momentum of an object over a period of time. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{L} = \Delta \mathbf{p}
$$

where $\mathbf{L}$ is the angular impulse and $\Delta \mathbf{p}$ is the change in angular momentum.

#### 2.3d.1 Conservation of Angular Momentum

The conservation of angular momentum is a fundamental principle in classical mechanics. It states that the total angular momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external angular impulse. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\sum \mathbf{p}_i = \sum \mathbf{p}_f
$$

where $\mathbf{p}_i$ is the initial angular momentum of the system and $\mathbf{p}_f$ is the final angular momentum of the system.

The conservation of angular momentum is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion, which states that the rate of change of angular momentum of an object is directly proportional to the applied angular impulse. If no external angular impulse is acting on a system, the angular momentum of the system remains constant.

#### 2.3d.2 Angular Impulse in Collision Problems

Collision problems are a common application of the concepts of angular impulse and angular momentum. In a collision, two or more objects interact for a short period of time, exchanging angular momentum. The conservation of angular momentum can be used to analyze these collisions.

Consider a system of objects with initial angular momenta $\mathbf{p}_1$, $\mathbf{p}_2$, ..., $\mathbf{p}_n$. If they collide and their final angular momenta are $\mathbf{p}_1'$, $\mathbf{p}_2'$, ..., $\mathbf{p}_n'$, the conservation of angular momentum can be written as:

$$
\mathbf{p}_1 + \mathbf{p}_2 + \cdots + \mathbf{p}_n = \mathbf{p}_1' + \mathbf{p}_2' + \cdots + \mathbf{p}_n'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the unknown final angular momenta after the collision.

#### 2.3d.3 Angular Impulse in Relativistic Mechanics

In relativistic mechanics, the concept of angular impulse is generalized to account for the effects of special relativity. The relativistic angular impulse is defined as the change in angular momentum divided by the square root of one minus the square of the ratio of the angular velocity of the object to the speed of light. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{L} = \frac{\Delta \mathbf{p}}{\sqrt{1 - \frac{v^2}{c^2}}}
$$

where $v$ is the angular velocity of the object and $c$ is the speed of light. This equation shows that the angular impulse of an object increases with its angular velocity, but only up to the speed of light. Beyond this speed, the angular impulse becomes infinite, which is a consequence of the special theory of relativity.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamental concepts of classical mechanics, focusing on the principles of motion and the laws of motion. We have delved into the concepts of force, mass, and acceleration, and how these factors interact to determine the motion of an object. We have also examined the laws of motion, including Newton's first and second laws, and how these laws govern the behavior of objects in motion.

We have also discussed the concept of momentum and how it is conserved in a closed system. This concept is crucial in understanding the behavior of objects in motion, particularly in collisions. We have also explored the concept of work and energy, and how these concepts are related to the concept of force.

Finally, we have discussed the concept of potential energy and how it is related to the concept of work. This concept is crucial in understanding the behavior of objects in motion, particularly in systems with gravitational potential energy.

In conclusion, classical mechanics is a fundamental branch of physics that deals with the motion of objects under the influence of forces. The principles and laws discussed in this chapter form the foundation for understanding more complex phenomena in physics, including the behavior of objects in motion.

## Chapter: Chapter 3: The Principle of Superposition

### Introduction

The principle of superposition is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics, and it forms the basis of many physical phenomena. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of this principle, providing a comprehensive understanding of its applications and implications.

The principle of superposition, as the name suggests, is a principle that allows us to break down complex systems into simpler, more manageable parts. In classical mechanics, this principle is often used to analyze systems with multiple forces acting on them. By considering each force acting on the system separately, we can then sum up the effects of all these forces to determine the overall behavior of the system.

Mathematically, the principle of superposition can be expressed as:

$$
\sum_{i} \mathbf{F}_i = 0
$$

where $\mathbf{F}_i$ are the forces acting on the system. This equation represents the condition for equilibrium, which is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics.

In this chapter, we will explore the principle of superposition in detail, discussing its applications in various physical systems. We will also delve into the concept of equilibrium and its importance in classical mechanics. By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of the principle of superposition and its role in classical mechanics.




#### 2.3b Impulse and Momentum Change

Impulse is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics that describes the change in momentum of an object. It is a vector quantity, just like momentum, and is defined as the difference between the final and initial momentum of an object. Mathematically, it can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \mathbf{p}_f - \mathbf{p}_i
$$

where $\mathbf{J}$ is the impulse, $\mathbf{p}_f$ is the final momentum, and $\mathbf{p}_i$ is the initial momentum.

#### 2.3b.1 Conservation of Momentum

The conservation of momentum is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion. It states that the rate of change of momentum of an object is directly proportional to the applied force. If no external force is acting on an object, the momentum of the object remains constant. This principle can be mathematically represented as:

$$
\frac{d\mathbf{p}}{dt} = \mathbf{F}
$$

where $\mathbf{F}$ is the external force acting on the object.

#### 2.3b.2 Impulse in Collision Problems

Collision problems are a common application of the concept of impulse. In a collision, two or more objects interact for a short period of time, exchanging energy and momentum. The conservation of momentum is a powerful tool in analyzing these collisions.

Consider a two-body collision where two objects of masses $m_1$ and $m_2$ are moving with velocities $\mathbf{v}_1$ and $\mathbf{v}_2$ respectively. If they collide and their final velocities are $\mathbf{v}_1'$ and $\mathbf{v}_2'$, the conservation of momentum can be written as:

$$
m_1 \mathbf{v}_1 + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2 = m_1 \mathbf{v}_1' + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the impulse delivered by the collision, which is given by:

$$
\mathbf{J} = m_1 (\mathbf{v}_1 - \mathbf{v}_1') + m_2 (\mathbf{v}_2 - \mathbf{v}_2')
$$

This equation shows that the impulse delivered by a collision is equal to the sum of the changes in momentum of the colliding objects. This is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion.

#### 2.3b.3 Impulse and Momentum Change

The relationship between impulse and momentum change is a fundamental concept in classical mechanics. As we have seen, impulse is defined as the change in momentum of an object. This means that the impulse delivered by a force is equal to the change in momentum of the object on which the force is acting. Mathematically, this can be represented as:

$$
\mathbf{J} = \Delta \mathbf{p}
$$

where $\mathbf{J}$ is the impulse, and $\Delta \mathbf{p}$ is the change in momentum.

This relationship is particularly useful in collision problems, where the impulse delivered by the collision is equal to the change in momentum of the colliding objects. This allows us to solve for the impulse delivered by the collision, as we have seen in the previous section.

In conclusion, impulse and momentum change are fundamental concepts in classical mechanics. They describe the change in momentum of an object and are essential tools in solving collision problems. The conservation of momentum, as a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion, provides a powerful framework for understanding these concepts.




#### 2.3c Conservation of Momentum

The conservation of momentum is a fundamental principle in classical mechanics that describes the relationship between the momentum of a system and the forces acting on it. It is a direct consequence of Newton's second law of motion and is expressed mathematically as:

$$
\frac{d\mathbf{p}}{dt} = \mathbf{F}
$$

where $\mathbf{p}$ is the momentum of the system, $\mathbf{F}$ is the total force acting on the system, and $\frac{d}{dt}$ denotes the derivative with respect to time.

#### 2.3c.1 Momentum as a Constant of Motion

In the absence of external forces, the total momentum of a system remains constant. This is known as the conservation of momentum. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\frac{d\mathbf{p}}{dt} = 0
$$

This principle is particularly useful in systems where the forces acting on the system are balanced, such as in a closed system where the total force is zero. In such cases, the total momentum of the system remains constant.

#### 2.3c.2 Collision Problems

Collision problems are a common application of the conservation of momentum. In a collision, two or more objects interact for a short period of time, exchanging energy and momentum. The conservation of momentum is a powerful tool in analyzing these collisions.

Consider a two-body collision where two objects of masses $m_1$ and $m_2$ are moving with velocities $\mathbf{v}_1$ and $\mathbf{v}_2$ respectively. If they collide and their final velocities are $\mathbf{v}_1'$ and $\mathbf{v}_2'$, the conservation of momentum can be written as:

$$
m_1 \mathbf{v}_1 + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2 = m_1 \mathbf{v}_1' + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the final velocities of the objects after the collision.

#### 2.3c.3 Multiple Collisions

In some cases, a system may undergo multiple collisions in a short period of time. The conservation of momentum can still be applied, but it is important to consider the total momentum of the system before and after each collision. The total momentum of the system is the sum of the momenta of all the objects in the system.

Consider a system of three objects with masses $m_1$, $m_2$, and $m_3$ moving with velocities $\mathbf{v}_1$, $\mathbf{v}_2$, and $\mathbf{v}_3$ respectively. If these objects collide and their final velocities are $\mathbf{v}_1'$, $\mathbf{v}_2'$, and $\mathbf{v}_3'$, the conservation of momentum can be written as:

$$
m_1 \mathbf{v}_1 + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2 + m_3 \mathbf{v}_3 = m_1 \mathbf{v}_1' + m_2 \mathbf{v}_2' + m_3 \mathbf{v}_3'
$$

This equation can be used to solve for the final velocities of the objects after the collisions.

#### 2.3c.4 Non-Inertial Reference Frames

The conservation of momentum is a local principle, meaning it applies to a system observed in a non-inertial reference frame. In such frames, the total momentum of a system can change due to the presence of non-conservative forces. However, the principle of conservation of momentum still holds when expressed in terms of the total momentum of the system.

Consider a system observed in a non-inertial reference frame. The total momentum of the system is given by:

$$
\mathbf{p} = m \mathbf{v}
$$

where $m$ is the mass of the system and $\mathbf{v}$ is the velocity of the center of mass of the system. The total force acting on the system is given by:

$$
\mathbf{F} = m \mathbf{a}
$$

where $\mathbf{a}$ is the acceleration of the center of mass of the system. Substituting these expressions into the equation for the conservation of momentum, we get:

$$
\frac{d\mathbf{p}}{dt} = m \frac{d\mathbf{v}}{dt} = m \mathbf{a} = \mathbf{F}
$$

This equation shows that the total momentum of the system changes at a rate equal to the total force acting on the system. This is the conservation of momentum expressed in terms of the total momentum of the system.

In conclusion, the conservation of momentum is a powerful tool in classical mechanics. It allows us to analyze systems of interacting objects, including systems undergoing multiple collisions and systems observed in non-inertial reference frames. By understanding the principles behind momentum conservation, we can gain a deeper understanding of the fundamental laws of classical mechanics.




#### 2.3d Collisions

Collisions are a fundamental concept in classical mechanics, and they are governed by the principles of conservation of momentum and energy. In this section, we will explore the different types of collisions and how to analyze them using the principles of classical mechanics.

#### 2.3d.1 Types of Collisions

Collisions can be broadly classified into two types: elastic and inelastic collisions. In an elastic collision, the total kinetic energy of the system is conserved, while in an inelastic collision, some of the kinetic energy is lost due to the conversion into other forms of energy, such as heat or sound.

#### 2.3d.2 Elastic Collision

In an elastic collision, the total momentum and kinetic energy of the system are conserved. This means that the total momentum of the system before the collision is equal to the total momentum after the collision. Similarly, the total kinetic energy of the system before the collision is equal to the total kinetic energy after the collision.

The final velocities of the objects after an elastic collision can be calculated using the following equations:

$$
v'_{1x} = \frac{v_{1}\cos(\theta_1-\varphi)(m_1-m_2)+2m_2v_{2}\cos(\theta_2-\varphi)}{m_1+m_2}\cos(\varphi)+v_{1}\sin(\theta_1-\varphi)\cos(\varphi + \tfrac{\pi}{2})
\\[0.8em]
v'_{1y} = \frac{v_{1}\cos(\theta_1-\varphi)(m_1-m_2)+2m_2v_{2}\cos(\theta_2-\varphi)}{m_1+m_2}\sin(\varphi)+v_{1}\sin(\theta_1-\varphi)\sin(\varphi + \tfrac{\pi}{2})
$$

where $v_{1x}$ and $v_{1y}$ are the x and y components of the velocity of the first object, $v_{2x}$ and $v_{2y}$ are the x and y components of the velocity of the second object, $m_1$ and $m_2$ are the masses of the objects, $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of the velocities of the objects, and $\varphi$ is the contact angle.

#### 2.3d.3 Inelastic Collision

In an inelastic collision, the total momentum and kinetic energy of the system are not conserved. This means that the total momentum of the system before the collision is not equal to the total momentum after the collision. Similarly, the total kinetic energy of the system before the collision is not equal to the total kinetic energy after the collision.

The final velocities of the objects after an inelastic collision can be calculated using the following equations:

$$
v'_{1x} = \frac{v_{1}\cos(\theta_1-\varphi)(m_1-m_2)+2m_2v_{2}\cos(\theta_2-\varphi)}{m_1+m_2}\cos(\varphi)+v_{1}\sin(\theta_1-\varphi)\cos(\varphi + \tfrac{\pi}{2})
\\[0.8em]
v'_{1y} = \frac{v_{1}\cos(\theta_1-\varphi)(m_1-m_2)+2m_2v_{2}\cos(\theta_2-\varphi)}{m_1+m_2}\sin(\varphi)+v_{1}\sin(\theta_1-\varphi)\sin(\varphi + \tfrac{\pi}{2})
$$

where $v_{1x}$ and $v_{1y}$ are the x and y components of the velocity of the first object, $v_{2x}$ and $v_{2y}$ are the x and y components of the velocity of the second object, $m_1$ and $m_2$ are the masses of the objects, $\theta_1$ and $\theta_2$ are the angles of the velocities of the objects, and $\varphi$ is the contact angle.

#### 2.3d.4 Angle-Free Representation

The equations for the final velocities of the objects after a collision can also be represented in an angle-free manner. This representation is useful when dealing with complex collisions involving multiple objects. The changed velocities are computed using the centers and at the time of contact as

$$
\mathbf{v}'_1 = \mathbf{v}_1-\frac{2 m_2}{m_1+m_2} \ \frac{\langle \mathbf{v}_1-\mathbf{v}_2,\,\mathbf{x}_1-\mathbf{x}_2\rangle}{\|\mathbf{x}_1-\mathbf{x}_2\|^2} \ (\mathbf{x}_1-\mathbf{x}_2),
\mathbf{v}'_2 = \mathbf{v}_2-\frac{2 m_1}{m_1+m_2} \ \frac{\langle \mathbf{v}_2-\mathbf{v}_1,\,\mathbf{x}_2-\mathbf{x}_1\rangle}{\|\mathbf{x}_2-\mathbf{x}_1\|^2} \ (\mathbf{x}_2-\mathbf{x}_1)
$$

where the angle brackets indicate the inner product (or dot product) of two vectors.

#### 2.3d.5 Other Conserved Quantities

In addition to momentum and energy, there are other quantities that are conserved in a collision. These include the total angular momentum and the total linear momentum. These quantities can be used to further analyze the collision and determine the final velocities of the objects.
